0525478817|5.0|NO SPOILERS<br /><br />It is hard to believe this novel is fiction. I was stunned at the realism of this novel and pulled in by how genuine the characters are and how much depth Green offered in what is a fairly simple story. If you are looking for high action and theatrics, this book has none. If you are looking for a real and \\"human\\" story, do not pass this one by. It is for both a younger and older (as I am) audience.<br /><br />First, do not assume this novel will be a downer since it is based on kids battling cancer. Yes, this is the backbone of the storyline; however, there's more to it than that. There are moments that will certainly provoke tears, but it is also laced with humor. Perhaps this is one thing the novel teaches us: humor is essential to our health and well-being. It is also a beautiful story of empathy and compassion. It reminds me of Atticus in To Kill a Mockingbird when he says, \\"You never truly understand a person until you walk around in their skin.\\" The characters in this novel had their assumptions challenged and learned of the interconnectedness of human nature.<br /><br />The novel is also an intelligent one. There are many allusions to other literary works (besides the one in the title) that enhance reader enjoyment and understanding of the characters. Even if you don't know what he's referencing, you can understand why Green included particular literary tidbits in the story.<br /><br />What is most refreshing is the sincerity and realism of the characters. Many YA novelists don't really know how young people think, behave, and talk and will slather on the, \\"Like, OMG, no way\\" or emo attitude as a means of capturing \\"true\\" adolescent behavior, which incorrectly over-generalizes teens as flakey and moody. Green's young characters are realistic. They have their occasional OMG and eyeball rolling moments, but, as a high school teacher, I can say that they are truly an accurate portrayal of their age group: sarcastic, sweet, sometimes selfish, intelligent, full of bravado and vulnerability among other traits. As a parent, I could also identify with the parents in the novel who simply needed to hover around their children watching terrible TV shows just for the mere pleasure of soaking up every available moment with them.<br /><br />Green covers so much ground in the book: love, loss, mortality, family, friendship, survival, dignity, and memory. It is a wothwhile read.

Tattoo was a little different at first and most didn't know what to make of it... Until what? Until they gave it a few listens and I can't tell you how many times I've heard people say or read reviews that it actually grew on them.  If ADKOT doesn't hit you between the numbers in the first 2-3 listens, it will...unless you've already made up your mind so like a lot of stuff, you really have to give it several listens before you can throw out a kneejerk opinion about it.<br />But if you are a trueVan Halen fan or even have a decent ear for good music, then you will not deny that this is simply the best Van Halen album since 1984 (I've heard some say ever, but I'm not  gonna go that far)-but it's certainly up there where they left off when Diamond Dave got dillusions of Grandeur & went Crazy from the Heat' [By the way:  As crazy as it seemed at the time, DLR's 1st 2 solo albums speak for themselves sporting the best Rock guitarist and Bassist even to this day in Steve Vai & Billy Sheehan...talk about masterpieces...both are!]<br />Also, so much is being made about rehashing older songs...yes, I heard `She's the Woman' back in the day and thought it was OK, but so many have not heard it and VH has  breathed new life into it while bringing it more to the masses-Yes, Tattoo is an older song called `Down in Flames', but only in it's arrangement...the song wasn't really that good...until Dave laid down a brilliant new vocal melody and in my humble opinion, doing more than breathing new life into an older cut as it really is a completely different song.  I think so many try to spit out these comparisons to say: I was there back when, and I know something you don't... WHO CARES people...who really cares?! Give them credit for getting it done already - it's been 28 years since they released a full album(CD)with Dave and released 13-strong cuts...I don't care if they wrote them when they were in High School, I say better late than never!<br />It should be about the strength of the songs and not(in a baby- whining voice)...'they had 7 of these songs from way back in the late 70's...whaaaa, whaaa' -Whoop-tee-doo! I have never met a person who loves Life is Beautiful ever say a bad thing about Schindler's List.  They are two different films, with two different purposes.  Schindler's List exposes the cold, dark reality of 1940s Europe and teaches about the Holocaust on a mass scale.  However, if we look at history, we can see that people often can't see the forest for the trees.  In Rwanda, it's hard to hear that millions died, but it's even more touching to see Hotel Rwanda and what one man did for one group of people.  The same is the case here.  Life is Beautiful is not about the 6 million Jews who died; it is a fable about ONE family's struggle to survive.  The impact is that rather than just another faceless person dying, you see Guido, a man that the viewer has developed an extreme closeness and affection for, face the end.  In that way, Life is Beautiful is actually far more analogous to The Pianist.<br /><br />Third, anyone who writes a review stating that they only watched the first half of the movie has no idea what they're talking about.  THIS IS NOT A COMEDY ABOUT THE HOLOCAUST.  That is an insult.  The first half is jovial and light because it is about the family BEFORE the dark cloud of the Holocaust took over their lives.<br /><br />Fourth, the comedy that appears in the second half is there for one purpose: survival.  Guido loves his son so much that he is willing to do anything to protect him.  Anything.  That is not comedy.  That is not insulting. Michael Pitt as Jimmy Darmody is equally magnetic, following the Michael Corleone example of the academic war hero who gets led into the underworld of crime and finds himself quite adept at it. In all, \\"Boardwalk Empire\\" is a pleasure for the senses that tells more about the 1920'a than a dozen history books. Their folk melodies are extraordinary. The death metal aspects are brutal and rhythmical. Combining both was a brilliant move. They stay away from the silliness that unfortunately characterizes most folk metal. Eluveitie is one of a kind.<br /><br />As a side note, Eluveitie is one of the very best live acts around. I highly recommend seeing their shows.

0345522478|2.0|I'm writing this review halfway through _Hounded: The Iron Druid Chronicles_ because I'm debating whether or not to continue reading. The gist of the book is clear already, and I get the hollow feeling anything that follows will be more of the same.<br /><br />A two-millennia-old druid from the Emerald Isle, Atticus O'Sullivan, has upset the gods by stealing a sword no armor can resist. In doing so, he foiled plans for Irish ascendency and had a Celtic bounty placed on his head by the god of love, Aenghus &Oacute;g. Aided by the goddess of death and a penchant for loading up on fae-destroying iron implements (hence his moniker, the Iron Druid), O'Sullivan hides out in his occult bookstore near a college campus and acts the part of a 20-ish slacker while he waits for the final battle.<br /><br />What author Hearne has created in O'Sullivan is a poorly disguised clone of Jim Butcher's notable wizard/detective, Harry Dresden. Add elements from Neil Gaiman's _American Gods_, and be prepared to walk down familiar paths.<br /><br />And familiar paths done better by Butcher and Gaiman. While _Hounded_ is a definite improvement over the writing of Simon Green in that author's Butcher/Gaiman-esque \\"Nightside\\" series, Hearne's technically adept writing chops can't mask glaring fan-fiction errors. Everything about his Atticus O'Sullivan is too perfect. Whereas Harry Dresden struggles with women, O'Sullivan effortlessly beds every female who crosses his path, minor deities included. Whereas Dresden has a dog that seems to anticipate his moves, O'Sullivan communicates telepathically and carries on snarky conversations with his pooch. Whereas Dresden often botches or underestimates his magic, O'Sullivan easily fools the gods with his. And while Harry Dresden is a sympathetic character with real flaws, O'Sullivan had me rooting for the bad guys to wipe the smirk off his smug face.<br /><br />These \\"too much, too soon\\" characterization and storyline flaws are typical in fan faction. I'm convinced that Hearne can write better than this, which makes _Hounded_ so disappointing. Rather than trying to be a Jim Butcher clone writing a \\"better, cooler\\" Harry Dresden, he should have found a way to create distance and develop a voice, a style, and characters that set him apart.<br /><br />The lack of originality in current fantasy is troubling, with too many authors writing derivative works. When a fantasy author cobbles together character types in a desperate search to create something new, such as Hearne does with his Viking warrior vampire lawyer, the race to the bottom finds another gear. Where are the unique voices, writing styles, tones, characterizations, settings, and so on? And why do so many of the newly published authors in science fiction and fantasy write like fan-fiction writers? Haven't they been to a writer's workshop before? And are publishers so desperate to make a buck that they'll sign up these folks just to ride some better author's coattails? If so, readers of good sf/fantasy will be wading through a lot of trash and not finding much treasure.<br /><br />That said, the outrageous number of 5-star reviews for _Hounded_ tells me that publishers have diluted the offerings so well that readers can no longer discern between originality and mimicry. Or readers have made peace with sucking down derivatives while they wait between releases of Dresden novels--or whatever their favorite series and author might be. Either way, it's a sad state of affairs, and Hearne's _Hounded_ is the poster child.convinced that Hearne can write better than this, which makes _Hounded_ so disappointing. 1594203334|2.0|I applaud the effort, and I was looking forward to reading this after hearing her on NPR and reading a couple of reviews.  It seemed like the perfect thing to read as a half Francophile/half-French mom who's certainly not averse to suggestions on how to expand her children's tastes and good behaviors. That said, this book is a fantasy and a significant misrepresentation of parenting, let alone French parenting.  As someone whose entire family still lives in Paris, I'd say one thing you end definitely up feeling among French people is that your \\"Americanisms\\" are \\"taches\\" (strikes against you) that can't usually be fixed.  This fact alone could have been Druckerman's entree into making French culture accessible, but she only chronicles it and leaves the reader feeling low.<br />It's at times humorous, if you look at it through the right lens, but Druckerman seems to take 90% of her time lamenting about how unfortunate it is that she's got such \\"terrible\\" American habits, holding up zero pride in who she actually is and where she comes from. I'm not an overzealous patriot, but take some pride!  She talks about all the \\"whiny brats\\" she encounters upon occasionally re-entering New York, which I must say, is almost as common in France, though it is met with far more spankings and coldness from their parents.<br />What she doesn't spend any time talking about is how such an upbringing (I feel I can say this as a French person) risks producing the grown version of many of these French children, Parisians in particular: people who are rude, self-centered, jealous (taught in school with an emphasis on intense academic competition and public ridicule for those who do not excel), intolerant, and quite frequently, the opposite of kind. She does not address the effects or outcome of a culture who does not allow their children to express themselves freely and frequently.  She also fails to draw the conclusion that these are the very adults who look down their noses at those whoneither know nor follow their secret rules for raising children.<br />Since the majority of us don't have the time or the luxury to decode these mores, it's a real shame that she gave such a snobby, inaccessible version of French parenting culture to the masses.  She claims at the beginning to not even really like France, and it seems she took that as license to only draw superficial conclusions.  I keep waiting for the book to get better, as I'm 2/3 through it, but I felt I needed to put in my review before other mothers purchase this book and end up feeling as I currently do:<br />If you are a mother whose time is extremely precious, I would recommend devoting it to a book that will not make you feel guilty and disappointed in yourself for turning out children who laugh a lot, smile a lot, play happily but yes, interrupt from time to time and prefer short meals and the company of their peers.  Would I love it if my children (3 and 1 yr) could sit peacefully through a meal and demand fines herbes on their endive salad? Absolutely! That's why I bought the book!  But there's so little HOW in here that I've not been able to justify the remainder.  I know it's easy to critique from afar, and I wish I could say better things in this one and only book review I've ever written, but alas, I can't.

0897500482|5.0|There are quite a number of reasons that I would recommend this book. The first is that regardless of what you may think of him, Bruce Lee did achieve the status of \\"Legend\\". This is a rare glimpse into his though processes. You have to understand also that this is not a book that Bruce Lee \\"wtote\\" but rather a book that was compiled after his death based upon numerous notes, drawings, scribblings etc that Lee kept in his private journals and other places.<br /><br />Any true fan of Bruce's would consider it a treasure even moreso than if he had actually written it with the intent of marketing it as a book. I first purchased this book many years ago while in high school and it truly revolutionized my thinking as a martial artist and also let me know that I was on the right track in my personal critique of various martial arts and my innate desire to immediately modify whatever I was then learning as a novice in order to better suit ME and my personal goals and objectives.<br /><br />Just know that this is NOT a training manual or guide although it has lots of training info and guidance that you can easily apply to ANY martial art (as well as other things that you might be pursuing). Just know that Bruce Lee was a philosophy major in college so it contains a heavy does of his personal philosophies on things pertaining to martial arts.<br /><br />There are sections that give a good glimpse into the actual WAY of Jeet Kune Do which I look at as much more of a CONCEPT than an actual martial ART. It is a concept that anyone can apply and it is based upon what many martial artists had to learn the hard way from the Gracies Ultimate Challenge matches and the UFC. Bruce was ahead of his time inasmuch as what has now evolved into what we call MMA was the very idea of Bruce's philosophy of Jeet Kune Do. MMA is NOT a \\"system\\" or a \\"style\\" but rather a WAY of combat based upon combing \\"whatever is useful\\" to get the job done and \\"discarding that which is not useful\\". And the complete avoidance of a ritualized, over organized one-size-fits-all approach to combat.<br /><br />Also, Bruce practiced what he preached training with and both learning from and also teaching greats like Joon Rhee; Gene Lebell; Joe Lewis; Chuck Norris; Ed Parker; Wally Jay and many other's. So for all of these reasons this book is a valuable addition to the library of any martial artist. It is not so much a book that you READ as much as it is a book that you STUDY.<br /><br />Each reader will find different things within the context of this book that resonate with them. One of the things that this book did for me was cause me to invest serious research into developing a system of fighting from the ground and also develop different plans of action for various real combat scenario's.<br /><br />I'd give it more than 5 stars if I could for all of these reasons.

Skeeter changes names for anonymity and Aibileen eventually agrees to let Skeeter interview her--then Minnie agrees. B0029LHWFO|5.0|Review of Dreams from my Father~<br /><br />This autobiographical narrative \\"Dreams from my Father\\" by Barrack Obama, gives a compelling tale of our President's life prior to being a student at Harvard Law School.  Resilience, race, inheritance, are all words Obama uses to describe his life while being raised by a white single mother and his maternal grandparents in Hawaii, and the longing he felt for his father who lived in Kenya. In his high school years, Obama learns a lifestyle of \\"partying\\", drugs, and alcohol. He then comes to the realization that there are certain expectations of him from his family such as getting a great education, defining his own success, and being an upstanding citizen. While in college, Obama began to witness poverty fist hand, and experience racism. This eventually compelled him to focus on how he could make a \\"change\\". From that moment, he finally began to see himself as a man that his father had always dreamed of. Obama graduated magna cum laude from Harvard Law School, just as he received the frightening news of his father's unexpected death. Obama never felt the close, father-son connection that he idealized from his father. Following the death of his father, he felt that it was crucial for him to travel to his father's native Kenya. This journey not only helped Obama connect with his paternal roots, but also helped him forge the father-son bond that he had always been longing for from his now deceased father. Indeed, Obama's paternal relatives welcomed him with open arms, and celebrated the return of their native son.<br /><br />The entire novel revolves around cultural identity. Ironically, even though Obama was born biracial, and raised in a white household, he identifies exclusively with his African heritage. From his childhood memories in Hawaii, to the urban life of Chicago, to the buildings of Harvard, and finally in grasslands of Kenya, Obama never lost his self-identity in a society that demanded so much of him.<br /><br />Barrack Obama's graceful and cadence style of writing also offers the reader a preview of his current leadership as the president. He writes from such an intriguingly rich perspective that each sentence is as compelling as it is profound; \\"Dreams from my father\\" diffidently establishes Obama's talent and competence both in literacy and the political sphere. In this book, Obama persuades the reader very convincingly with his own meaning of education, power, resilience, and success by captivating the reader with words that will remain with you long after you've finished the book:  \\"Away from my mother, away from my grandparents, I was engaged in a fitful interior struggle. I was trying to raise myself to be a black man in America, and beyond the given of my appearance, no one around me seemed to know exactly what that meant.\\"<br /><br />While reading this memoir, I felt an instant connection between myself and Obama. Whether someone can relate with Obama's reality as an African-American or not, his memoir is absolutely spellbinding for anyone. In this never put-down, climatic, autobiography, our president takes the reader on a personal journey where the final stop leaves you with a very inspirational outlook on finding success in America.

B005K0HDGE|5.0|I've been a fan of Stephen King for a long time. It's hard to remember the first novel that I read, but it was either Christine or The Stand (way back in the early 1980's). I was hooked. What's always enthralled me are the characters he creates, who seem like real, live, breathing people that you've met in your own life. 11/22/63 doesn't disappoint in that category. And it's clear that Steve and the folks that support him have done their research; they quite adeptly portray life as it was in the late 50's and early 60's (at least, from what I've heard - I didn't come along until the mid 60's). And I learned a fair amount about Lee Harvey Oswald, particularly about his personal life. I knew he had defected to Russia and returned to the U.S. only a few years later. I knew he was a big fan of Fidel Castro. And I knew his wife was Russian. I didn't know he had children, nor did I know that he apparently beat his wife fairly regularly.<br /><br />This definitely isn't a horror novel. In having the 'obdurate past' that resists the protagonist's attempts to change what happened, King created a rather unique 'villain'. But the plot follows a familiar structure for Steve, in particular the requirement that the protagonist must sacrifice something they care about to win the day. But, I'm willing to overlook that because he's given us an engaging tale that makes you want to read it NOW to get to the end. And what an ending! It didn't entirely surprise me but if your eyes are completely dry at the end, you're not human. Although I guess Jake's would be (you have to read the book to understand that one!).<br /><br />There are a few minor quibbles. Steve clearly doesn't like Dallas. I lived in Dallas for 5 years and would happily do so again. Was it paradise? No, like any town it has issues. But it was a great town to live in; lots to see & do, cheap to live in, and filled with friendly Texans. I also would take issue with his tirade in the Afterword about extremism. Steve, you're right that it can lead to violence, but please point out that it goes both ways. Political extremism on EITHER end of the spectrum can lead to violence and tragedy. Yes, it's a problem in our society today but please don't act as though it's only one group/side that's doing it!<br /><br />Are those quibbles enough for me to downgrade this book? No. Steve's allowed to project his political views and to even infuse them into his writing. To be frank, had he not mentioned the subject in his Afterword I wouldn't have said boo about it; the book itself is not promoting a political agenda. It is an honest portrayal of the U.S. in 1958. (SPOILER) You might be tempted to argue with his 'predictions' of what would have happened had Kennedy not died but I was happy that it wasn't at all the baby boomer fantasy that Vietnam/Nixon/etc... wouldn't have happened if Kennedy had lived.<br /><br />Bottom line: Best time travel book I've ready since \\"To Say Nothing of the Dog\\" by Connie Willis. Which if you haven't read, you absolutely should.

B006OGMEUI|5.0|Eluveitie<br />Helvetios<br />A non-historian's take on this concept album<br /><br />Helvetios is a Gaulish/Celtic tribe that used to inhabit the region of Gaul that is now Switzerland, and some of modern-day France. It tells the story of the Gaulish War from the perspective of this particular tribe.<br /><br />1 - Prologue<br />It is years after the Gaulish War and an old man laments those terrible years, what he and his people endured, and how they remember.<br /><br />2 - Helvetios<br />This song describes the rise of the Helvetians. How they became a distinct sect of people from other Gauls. It depicts some of their beliefs, their deities (Succellus, Ogmios, Bricta), and a bit of how their afterlife (Antumnos) plays a role in their lives.<br /><br />3 - Luxtos<br />Luxtos portrays Helvetios for what they were: rich, free, wild, and strong. This is the calm before the storm, the so-called \\"golden age\\" as it was for them when life was about just living, being happy, and enjoying oneself.<br /><br />4 - Home<br />This is an account of the time when the Romans were encroaching on Helvetios. The Gauls had to leave their home and find a new one to avoid being swept into war. On their journey they consider going back, but realize that just isn't possible and they must press on.<br /><br />5 - Santonian Shores<br />Continuing from the previous song, Santonian Shores recounts another event as Helvetios flees the Romans drive them from their land, again. The storyteller looks back and sees his village burning as they wandered into the night in search of a safe haven, leaving their past lives behind.<br /><br />6 - Scorched Earth<br />As the Helvetians travel onward, in search of a new home, they take a look back, the night sky colored red by the fires that was once their home. They are desperate and cold, but they can't turn back, they March on.<br /><br />7 - Meet the Enemy<br />It took the Helvetians twenty days to cross the River Saone, once Julius Caesar learned where they were, he attacked at night while they were burdened, crossing the river.  A quarter of the Helvetians died here. It was this attack against them that changed their viewpoint, and they knew they would have to fight. This was the first major battle of the Gaulish Wars.<br /><br />8 - Neverland<br />The second major battle of the Gaulish Wars was fought at Bibracte. The Helvetians managed to organize a sneak attack on the Roman Legions here, trying to take their baggage train. Ceasar caught wind and retaliated, inflicting severe damage to the Helvetians, driving 130,000 of them to flee, and the rest to die or surrender.<br /><br />9 - A Rose for Epona<br />Epona, the Gaulish goddess of Cavalry, was offered a rose for her protection. This song is about a young Gaulish woman, whom while fleeing the Romans with her kinsmen prays to Epona.  She asks her why she has abandoned them in their most desperate times, and offers a rose for her protection.<br /><br />10 - Havoc<br />This catalogs various attacks against Helvetios by the Romans.  How the Romans were never satisfied and always wanted more, stripping Gaul of it's resources to feed the Roman war machine at the cost of the Gaulish people's blood.<br /><br />11 - The Uprising<br />Finally, the Gaulish people have had enough. This is a call for all Gaulish Tribes to unite against the Roman Legions. To fight for freedom, so that they may once again know peace and not war.<br /><br />12 - Hope<br />This is an instrumental.<br /><br />13 - The Siege<br />The Gauls were losing the war still, so it was time to adopt a new strategy. The united tribes adopted a Scorched Earth tactic and set to deny Caesar of any resources that he may use against them.  The tribes swept the land and any town within the reach of Caesar's army was raised to the ground, denying him much needed supplies.  Avaricum was not, however, burned down, it was thought to be impenetrable. Caesar proved to break Avaricum's defenses his men pillaged, sacked, and raped the town.  Only 800 Gauls of 40,000 survived the siege turning Avaricum into a mass grave.<br /><br />14 - Alesia<br />After Avaricum, the Gaulish Tribes were scattered, leading small cavalry raids against the Romans.  This was not effective and their leader, Vercingetorix called a regroup at the hill-top fort of Alesia. Caesar once again caught on and decided that because Alesia was so well fortified a frontal assault would be a futile effort.  Instead, he built two walls around Alesia completely encompassing the fort, cutting the 80,000 Gauls inside off from supplies - intending to starve them out. Vercingetorix tried to negotiate the release of all the women and children in Alesia so only the fighters remained, but the Romans did not agree, leaving everyone inside to starve.  The deaths of wives and children decreased moral, but a few Gauls managed to escape and send word to the other tribes; in their most desperate hour, the relief force arrived, boosting morale for those trapped within to urge them to fight for another day. Under attack from both sides of the wall, the Romans in desperate measure flanked the relief forces to try to stop them.  It worked, it scrambled the Gauls and they were slaughtered. Witnessing the destruction of their only hope of escape, Vercingetorix surrendered the following day to Julius Caesar. This marked the end of the Gaulish Wars.<br /><br />15 - Tullianum<br />A Tullianum is an underground prison used by the Romans.  After his defeat at Alesia, Vercingetorix was taken prisoner and paraded fro five years around the lands as a monument to Caesar's tactics and victories over the Gauls. He was then taken to the Tullianum and strangled by rope.<br /><br />16 - Uxellodunon<br />Uxellodunon was the last attempt by the Gauls to defend a town against Roman assault. It was well defended, mountainous crags on one side, and rivers on the other. A natural spring on the mountain side suppled fresh water, along with the river on the the other side of town. The Romans, again, decided to cut off supply lines to the town, hoping to starve them, but soon realized they would need to remove their water supply as well.  Setting up siege engines and archers along the river effectively cut off the river as a water supply. The natural spring was harder, but the Romans constructed a ten-story tower filled with archers to fire at anyone using the spring.  The Gauls managed to attack the tower, trying to burn it to the ground.  Caesar then staged a false attack at the gates which made the Gauls withdraw their troops to the walls for defense, allowing the Romans to save the siege tower.  Getting nowhere, the Romans began to dig into the mountain and eventually diverted the natural spring, effectively drying it up.  The Gauls surrendered to the Romans.  Instead of being sold as slaves, Caesar ordered all the survivor's hands cut off and then set free. This was considered lenient and acted as a message to all other Gauls.<br /><br />17 - Epilogue<br />We end with a soft-spoken Gaulish man recounting the years of the war, and how it will be remembered.ed, and raped the town. B005HJWWW8|5.0|I'm not an audiophile, however I am what I'd call a \\"discerning listener\\". I'm not out to spend $1000 on headphones and buy all my music on CD to rip in lossless format, but I do like my music to be recreated as accurately as I can manage. My general listening is done on the MOG service, which streams in 320kbps MP3 format. Not the highest fidelity out there, but certainly high enough to notice problems with the drivers.<br /><br />The headphones I use, depending on situation, are either Etymotic HF3 (in-ear buds) or Shure SRH-840's (studio monitors), through my Asus Zenbook laptop. The difference between with it and without it are night and day. What you need to realize is that if you turn your computer up to 95-100% volume, it's driving at max capacity, leading to clipping and distortion. Throw an amp on, and suddenly all that clarity and fullness comes through at proper quality, without having to push your device to its maximum capability.<br /><br />I've bought a lot of different audio products over the years, and the E6 was the first that I'd consider truly indispensable. I also clip it onto my shorts at the gym while I listen to music through my Galaxy Nexus smartphone, and whereas normally I'd need to have it turned up the whole way to get the music loud enough, with the amp on I sit around 50-60%, and don't have to worry about the quality dropping. To me, the amp isn't just about making the sound louder, although that's a factor. It's also about being able to make it loud enough without a drop in quality.<br /><br />The battery lasts long enough. I just keep a cable plugged in and whenever I'm not using it, I plug it in. There ARE the multiple settings, but I find them wholly unnecessary. The bass boost sounds, frankly, terrible. With ANY music, that boost makes it muddy and difficult to listen to. The second setting (red light) apparently boosts the mids, which does nothing useful, and the final (purple) setting turns it down a bit. I assume this is for the VERY high end drivers, but I've found no use for it myself. I don't want any artificial EQ going on, that's not what an amp should do. If I want a bass boost I can do that in software.<br /><br />Point being, buy this. It's phenomenal.VERY high end drivers, but I've found no use for it myself. I don't want any artificial EQ going on, that's not what an amp should do. If I want a bass boost I can do that in software.<br /><br />Point being, buy this. It's phenomenal.

B003ZSH606|4.0|After letting myself digest Jamiroquai's latest album for a few months, I can say with confidence that Rock Dust Light Star, while it has some notable flaws, is well worth its price tag.  Let's start with the most obvious upside: the very first two tracks, \\"Rock Dust Light Star\\" and \\"White Knuckle Ride,\\" alone justify making this purchase.  \\"Rock Dust\\" is held together by a catchy guitar riff, and its vocals have a jaded, melancholy quality that's strangely compelling.  Although its lyrics are sometimes overly pretentious in their atheism (\\"I've never felt I ever prayed / Don't have to join in with the other slaves\\")--and I say this as an atheist myself--they are, in other cases, strikingly clever and memorable (\\"I'm a square pig in a round hole / Maybe there's no place left for my wounded soul / Maybe the King of Kings finally lost control of it\\").  Meanwhile, \\"White Knuckle Ride\\" has a smooth but hectic electronic feel that will readily stick in your head; it is not without reason that this was chosen as the first single from the album.  Both of these two songs are immediately catchy, yet durable, such that they have not become irritating or tiresome to me after many subsequent listens.<br /><br />Unfortunately, after this fantastic start, the songs begin to lag a bit.  There are a few other excellent ones, make no mistake: \\"Hurtin',\\" \\"Blue Skies,\\" and \\"She's a Fast Persuader\\" (the latter being a fairly unmistakable ode to oral sex) are particularly great; but these are counterbalanced by a few of the most cringe-worthy tracks that Jamiroquai has ever produced.  \\"Smoke and Mirrors\\" has my nomination for the worst single line in the album (\\"Well you're damned if you do / And you're damned if you don't / 'Cause your friends don't like his haircut / But he wants your lovin' tonight\\"), while \\"Two Completely Different Things\\" and \\"Never Gonna Be Another\\" strike me as being totally unforgettable and emotionally dry.  Still, my fullest revulsion is reserved for \\"Goodbyeto My Dancer\\" and \\"Hey Floyd,\\" which, whatever their upsides, make use of boring and emotionally uneven Caribbean instrumentation in a way that at once annoys me and puts me to sleep.<br /><br />I probably speak for the minority of my fellow long-time Jamiroquai fans when I say that Dynamite was my favorite album; I couldn't get enough of its hard, synthetic energy, as exemplified by tracks like \\"Electric Mistress,\\" \\"Black Devil Car,\\" and \\"Feels Just Like It Should.\\"  Rock Dust Light Star, the first real post-Dynamite release and also the first album that the band has produced without the constraints of their previous recording contract, has a much slower and more contemplative personality overall.  The quality of its lyrics has improved in some instances while deteriorating in others, and there's a noticeable absence of both experimentally vocal-free songs like \\"Didgin' Out\\" and politically-themed ones like \\"Virtual Insanity\\" or \\"Revolution 1993.\\"  (But then, I never really agreed with Jamiroquai's politics.)  When Rock Dust Light Star is bad, it's bad; but on the other hand, its strongest tracks are in some ways superior to my favorites from Dynamite.  I think that Jamiroquai fans and newcomers alike will be missing out if they don't get a hold of this album and enjoy those few engaging core songs.  Still, I do wish that the album were more of a complete experience, and that I didn't find myself routinely tempted to skip past more than half of its contents.<br /><br />Despite the tough love of my criticisms here, I would be remiss if I ended on a negative note.  Jamiroquai has been one of my favorite bands for perhaps a decade now; their catchy pop/dance/disco/funk/electronica sound, awesome vocals, and inventive wordplay distinguish them from the predictable and genre-bound artists who currently dominate the market.  Even at their worst, they are outstanding, and I'm not convinced that this is their worst title.  If you like Jamiroquai's work, buy Rock Dust LightStar with the understanding that it's something of a mixed bag.  If you have never listened to Jamiroquai, start by buying their last three albums, Dynamite, A Funk Odyssey, and Traveling Without Moving... and *then* buy Rock Dust Light Star.

0143036742|4.0|I happened upon this book at a bed & breakfast where my boyfriend and I were staying to celebrate his birthday. There wasn't time enough to finish it but by the time we left for home, sixty-five pages in, I was already hooked. Luckily, the book was available at my local library.<br /><br />For a comic novel, I didn't find it all that funny; I mean it wasn't a laugh riot. I'm certain I didn't laugh out loud even once.  I certainly didn't find myself rolling on the floor. \\"A Short History of Tractors in Ukrainian\\" is far more bittersweet than hilarious, even in its most hilarious moments.<br /><br />But that is what makes it a more powerful novel than you might be led to expect.<br /><br />The basic premise is this: An octogenarian widower decides to remarry. His two adult daughters are aghast. Their father is planning to marry a woman less than half his age, an opportunistic Ukrainian immigrant clearly looking to improve her station in life, enjoy all the material benefits of Western-style capitalism, and attain British citizenship for herself and her teenaged son.<br /><br />And Nikolai's daughters are right: Valentina is a tart with a criminal bent, a thoroughly rapacious creature looking to take Dad for all he's worth. The problem is that Nikolai sees it too...he simply doesn't see it, as do his more rational-minded daughters, as a sufficient reason not to marry the alluringly zaftig, more importantly, large-breasted Slavic blonde bombshell. For supremely rational as an engineer Nikolai may be, he is also a poet, a romantic, and a World War II survivor of both Stalin's communism and Hitler's fascism. He knows what it is to be desperate and terrified to escape a bad situation and he can't help but sympathize with Valentina's plight. And, of course, there are also the large breasts.<br /><br />Most of the humor in the book comes from Nikolai's almost Panglossian rationalization of Valentina's misdoings, his hope, in spite of his Schopenhauerian pessimism, that all will work out in the end, that love will prevail, that underneath those massive bosoms Valentina has a good heart.<br /><br />His feuding daughters, Nadia--the narrator of \\"A Short History\\"--and her big sister Vera are convinced of just the opposite and they form an uneasy alliance to oust Valentina first from their vulnerable father's life and, when that fails and things go from bad to worse to worst of all, get her thrown out of the country entirely. Nadia has always suspected there is more to her family's troubled history and their harrowing escape from the horrors of war-ravaged Europe than she's been told and she's right. Vera knows. Ten years older, she experienced it firsthand, but she won't divulge what it is. As she advises Nadia in rare moments of sisterly candor, the past is best left buried in the past.<br /><br />\\"A Short History of Tractors in Ukrainian\\" is a tragicomic novel in the way human life itself is both comedy and tragedy. You scratch the surface and it can tickle but beneath the tickle there can be a universe of pain. As funny as it is serious, as engagingly well written as it is compulsively readable, \\"A Short History\\" is a truly unique novel that manages to be informative, entertaining, moving, and, yes, humorous, all at the same time. I thoroughly enjoyed it.ill work out in the end, that love will prevail, that underneath those massive bosoms Valentina has a good heart.<br /><br />His feuding daughters, Nadia--the narrator of \\"A Short History\\"--and her big sister Vera are convinced of just the opposite and they form an uneasy alliance to oust Valentina first from their vulnerable father's life and, when that fails and things go from bad to worse to worst of all, get her thrown out of the country entirely. B0002TKFRM|3.0|My comments have mostly to do with the SACD sound, and not the performances here which on this disk are good, though I would not consider them the best or most \\"thrilling\\". After all, one buys a SACD not just for the performance, but for the sound.<br /><br />PERFORMANCES:<br />One may or may not have issues with these performances. Whatever your opinion on this aspect is, nevertheless, they are solid performances of well-known orchestral pieces.<br /><br />SONIC ISSUES:<br />For a SACD (Super Audio CD) one would think they are getting all the advantages the modern SACD format has to offer: extended frequency response, greater clarity, use of multiple channels, and more visceral impact due to the overall better sound capability that's intrinsic with the SACD format. Unfortunately such is not the case with this SACD.<br /><br />This disk is labeled SACD and certainly from a technical standpoint, the audio tracks on this disk are using the SACD format. However, where this disk fails to provide the sonic impact of a truly good SACD, in my opinion, stems from two factors:<br /><br />First, the performances on this SACD, were, according to the notes on the back jacket, originally recorded in three channels using front left, right, and center.  There are no other channels such as surround left/right, or surround rear left/right (applies to a 7.1 setup).<br /><br />So, whether you have a system set up as 5.1 surround or 7.1 surround, all your \\"surround\\" speakers will be totally silent with this disk.  The ONLY \\"surround\\" channel that WILL be active is the front center channel (and of course the front left and right (stereo) channels).<br /><br />The lack of better use of surround channels partly has to do with the era these performances come from: According to Wikipedia.com (do a search on \\"chicago symphony orchestra\\" at the Wikipedia.com site), the Chicago Symphony Orchestra under Fritz Reiner made their first RCA recording in 1954 and went on to make recordings up to 1962 using RCA's triple-channel \\"Living Stereo\\" process. And according to Wikipedia, RCA digitally remastered those recordings and re-released them as CD and SACD. So, from a technical standpoint, that piece of history explains why this SACD disk lacks any use of the surround channels simply because those channels did not exist in the original recordings.<br /><br />The RCA triple-microphone \\"Living Stereo\\" process is reminiscent of the Mercury \\"Living Presence\\" series that was very popular in the same period. Mercury Record's philosophy was to record performances in a very straightforward, clean way, without banks of microphones and hundred-channel mixers, so common with Columbia Records and the post-performance mixing and other electronic manipulation that was prevalent, and still done today.<br /><br />Similar to the RCA \\"Living Stereo\\" series, Mercury Records also used not more than three high quality microphones, strategically placed so as to capture an ideal combination of direct sound and the ambience of the hall. In fact, Mercury Records excelled in this type of recording which frequently surpassed the RCA Living Stereo records.<br /><br />I also own a good selection of both the original Mercury Living Presence vinyl disks and many of the same releases that were transferred to CDs.  I would add that not all of the CD were as successful sonically as the original vinyl versions. However, I mention Mercury records because of the similarity to the RCA Living Stereo philosophy.<br /><br />As with the RCA Living Stereo, the Mercury Living Presence recordings were landmarks for their day and still hold up very well, as do these RCA Living Stereo recordings, on today's audiophile equipment and speakers.<br /><br />More to the point, though, with this particular RCA Living Stereo recording, it would have been possible, during the digital remastering and transfer process for the SACD release, to derive ambiance information and utilize the surround channels for ambiance information.<br /><br />I suspect deriving ambience for the surround channels was not done either because it was not considered important at the time (perhaps not even considered), or perhaps because the existing recording, which hints at a certain amount of what sounds to be a bit of artificial ambiance in it's front channels, would have \\"detracted\\" from the finished product in the opinion of the engineers or producer.<br /><br />Whatever the reasons, it's hard to say why surround channels were not utilized in this remastered RCA SACD. And unfortunately, the back of the disk jacket gives no clues whatsoever regarding the remastering of these recordings.<br /><br />The second reason, in my opinion, has to do with the microphones available at the time. Good, high-quality mics certainly existed back in the 50's and 60's, but they can't compare to more modern mics in terms of frequency response and the incredible dynamic range they are capable of.<br /><br />Many of the compositions on this disk use the full range and scope of the symphony orchestra with heavy dependence on the percussion section. While the quality of the percussion, as recorded on this disk, is good, it does lack the impact and timbral accuracy better recordings of these compositions can demonstrate. Some select modern CD recordings of these same compositions render percussion with more accuracy than is conveyed on this disk.<br /><br />However, what is quite good on these transfers is the clarity of details in the musical texture and more importantly, a more realistic relative scale of instrumental sections, something that is frequently very badly conveyed with many recordings, even modern ones.<br /><br />For example, many regular CD recordings will fall short with how they convey a full brass section as compared to other sections. A lot of this \\"scaling\\" issue has to do with how the performers were recorded, where the mics were placed, and how the final mix-down was made all of which can dramatically alter, for better or worse, the final results.<br /><br />But such details are impossible to know just from listening, so one can only render a final determination on the basis of the final product and listening.<br /><br />MY RATING:<br />I gave this disk three stars out of a possible 5 because of the above issues. This is not a bad disk by any means and you will no doubt enjoy it. But I have these same compositions on regular CDs recorded with better modern recording methods and they are truly impressive with more visceral impact that this SACD provides.  Another SACD in the \\"Living Stereo\\" series available from Amazon,\\"Scheralazade\\", also performed by the Chicago Symphony, is considerably better sonically... if you have a good surround sound system and SACD player, that disk is one you definitely will want as part of your collection.I mention Mercury records because of the similarity to their recording philosophy. As with the RCA \\"Living Stereo\\", the Mercury \\"Living Presence\\" recordings were landmarks for their day and still hold up very well, as do these RCA \\"Living Stereo\\" recordings, on today's audiophile equipment and speakers.<br /><br />However, with respect to this particular RCA recording, it would have been possible during the digital remastering and transfer process to derive ambiance information and utilize the surround left and right and/or the surround rear left and right for that ambiance information. But I suspect it was not done either because it was not considered important at the time (or perhaps was not even considered), or perhaps because the existing recording, which hints at a certain amount of what sounds to be a bit of artificial ambiance in it's front channels, would have \\"detracted\\" from the finished product in the opinion of the engineers or producer. It's hard to say why without further information and the back of the disk jacket gives no clues whatsoever regarding the remastering of these recordings.<br /><br />The second reason stems more from the original recordings and what went into them in terms of the placement of the mics and more specifically, the actual mics used. Good, high-quality mics certainly existed back in the 50's and 60's but more modern mics, when properly placed, are able to pickup the impact of a bass drum, and percussion in general, in a way that mics from the 50s and 60s simply could not simply because they did not have a response down to 20 hz (and lower) as more modern mics are capable of.<br /><br />Many of the compositions on this disk use the full range and scope of the symphony orchestra with heavy dependence on the percussion section. While the quality of the percussion, as recorded on this disk, is good, it does lack the impact and timbrel accuracy better recordings of these compositions can demonstrate, even very good regular CD recordings render percussion with more accuracy than is conveyed on this disk.<br /><br />However, what is quite good on these transfers is the clarity of details in the music and a more realistic relative scale of instrumental sections. For example, many regular CD recordings will fall short with how they convey a full brass section as compared to other sections. A lot of this \\"scaling\\" issue has to do with how the performers were recorded; where the mics were placed, and how the final mix-down was made all of which can dramatically alter for better or worse the final results. But such details are impossible to know just from listening so one can only make a final judgement on the basis of the final product and listening. But there is a lot of detail in this disk in spite of what I consider an anemic and impact-less low-end.<br /><br />MY RATING:<br />I gave this disk three stars out of a possible 5 because of the above issues. This is not a bad disk by any means and you will no doubt enjoy it. But I have these same compositions on regular CDs recorded with better modern recording methods and they are truly impressive with more visceral impact that this SACD provides.  Another SACD in the \\"Living Stereo\\" series available from Amazon,\\"Scheralazade\\", also performed by the Chicago Symphony, is considerably better soncally... if you have a good system and SACD that disk is one you definitely should get.

0143038419|5.0|Eat, Pray, Love by Elizabeth Gilbert is, simply put, one of the best books that I have ever read. It is one of those rare books that I can read again and again, always finding some new inspiration or bit of humor, often in the same sentence. I first read it a few years ago, and recently watched the movie - which is actually quite good - and decided to read it again before reading the sequel, Committed.<br /><br />This is an everywoman's memoir. Ms. Gilbert experiences a wide range of emotions through the course of the book, and discovers that it is ok to fully experience all of them. After a long, painful divorce and the equally painful ending of a great love affair, she decides to take a year to experience the extremes of pleasure and complete devotion and to then try to find a balance between the two. She does this by living four months in what she affectionately calls \\"the three I's:\\" Italy, India, and Indonesia.<br /><br />Gilbert, for no practical reason that she can name, has always wanted to learn Italian simply for the beauty of the language. So she begins to study the language and discovers that she loves it, and she also discovers that when you find things that make you feel happy, you should not feel guilty or selfish about it. From this love for the language comes a decision to visit the country that is known for its indulgence in all things pleasure, especially food. And during her four months in Rome, Gilbert learns how to loosen up the ties of American Puritanism and actually allow herself to fully enjoy the language, the food, and the friends she makes there.<br /><br />The second stop of her journey is her Guru's ashram in India. This takes her from one end of the spectrum to the other as she begins a quiet period of meditation and prayer. At the ashram, they get up before dawn to chant, meditate, and pray before breakfast. Then they spend the rest of the day in spiritual studies and selfless work around the ashram (Gilbert is assigned floor scrubbing duties), with more praying and meditation thrown in as well. They eat a vegetarian diet that is quite different from the rich pastas and desserts that she enjoyed in Italy. But this life of devotion allows her to explore both the highest and lowest points of her existence, and also to connect with God, the divine love that is both within and without.<br /><br />Finally, Gilbert travels to the small island of Bali in Indonesia. She is actually returning there to study with Ketut Liyer, a medicine man she met two years earlier while in the midst of her divorce. He predicted many of the things that came to pass in her life, and also predicted that she would return to Bali. This leg of her journey is her attempt to reconcile the two extremes that she had already experienced and find a balance between the two. And she discovers that with the help of Ketut, a traditional Balinese healer and divorcee named Wayan, and a very self-indulgent group of expats, a balance can be forged between the two. She also blessed with the gift of love, and the courage to fully embrace it without giving up her self.<br /><br />While in Italy, Gilbert and her friends have a conversation about how each city and each person have their own words. For Rome, the word is sex, for Stockholm it is conform, and for New York it is achieve. After much thought, Gilbert finds that her word is antevasin, a Sanskrit word meaning \\"one who lives at the border.\\" As clichd as it may sound, the word for the book Eat, Pray, Love is inspiration. I feel a connection to Elizabeth Gilbert in that I have always thought there must be some sort of middle ground between the pleasures of life and the blessings of God (a middle ground that many religions would deny exists). I have struggled with depression and guilt between the two extremities for years. I have felt too guilty and self-conscious to indulge in the things that bring me even a small measure of joy. But through her studies, experiences, and unique brand of spirituality, Gilbert has given me inspiration and hope, and has taught me that \\"when you sense a faint potentiality for happiness after such dark times you must grab onto the ankles of that happiness and not let go until it drags you face-first out of the dirt - this is not selfishness, but obligation. You were given life; it is your duty (and also your entitlement as a human being) to find something beautiful within life, no matter how slight.\\" I haven't figured out what my word is yet, but after reading this beautifully moving memoir, I think I am well on my own journey to finding it.

055357342X|5.0|A Storm of Swords is part 3 in George R.R. Martin's series A Song of Ice and Fire. And while the first two books, A Game of Thrones and A Clash of Kings were engaging enough to keep you reading to the end and then picking up the next book, they are just \\"good\\" books(as in  3 or 3  stars) . A Storm of Swords, however, is where Martin comes into his own as a master of the epic saga (5 stars).<br /><br />The book continues in the same style as the first two, with each chapter being told by a different character. In the earlier books, this style slowed them down by making them somewhat confusing to follow at times. But in A Storm of Swords, it actually helps to propel the book forward and difficult to put down as the end of each chapter leaves you breathless with anticipation for that character's next chapter to come up. Then by the end of the following chapter, you are just as breathless over that character. This is one of those rare books that you could just sit and read in one sitting, if it weren't 1100 pages long (in paperback) and you didn't have any interruptions. This installment in the series is set apart because Martin has finally mastered the art of parallels - the plots and timelines of the various characters are woven together in a much tighter mesh, and the parallels between them are simply stunning. I love how Martin can take something as simple as a song (most memorably The Bear and the Maiden Fair) and by simply having it sung by various characters throughout the book remind you of earlier chapters. And while it is short on romance, this book contains elements of every genre imaginable - historical fiction, sci-fi/fantasy, humor, and even horror. I found myself crying, laughing, shrinking in horror at some of the more brutal scenes, and trembling with fear over the scary parts. Martin also does a magnificent job of turning the different story lines on their heads and keeping you guessing. The plots are anything but predictable as established, well-liked characters are killed off and more minor characters are brought to the forefront. There are even times when you find that you are suddenly sympathetic towards what originally seemed to be very unlikable characters.<br /><br />I simply cannot think of words to describe how exceptional this book is. As far as the series goes, you really wouldn't want to read it without having read the first two, but if you can make it through them (each book is massive with over 700 pages in the hardback editions) then you will fly through this one. I don't know if I can stand the wait until I can get the next book, A Feast for Crows.

B006QVRVJK|4.0|Adapted from an acclaimed play by Yasmin Reza, Roman Polanski's film CARNAGE is a darkly humourous tale of two happily married couples being gradually unmasked. After their son whacks another boy on the playground, Kate Winslet and Christoph Waltz visit Jodie Foster and John C. Reilly. What is meant to be a brief social call, where the parents discuss their children's conflict amicably, turns into a hell for everyone involved. In spite of their goodbyes and promises to arrange a later meeting, Winslet and Waltz can't seem to leave. And every time they are called back to a cup of coffee or a piece of cake, the tension in the room is higher and higher. CARNAGE expresses Sartre's view that hell is other people, but it also shows that hell is ourselves when we can't manage to get away from a stressful situation and regain composure.<br /><br />This is an elegant film which retains the simplicity and intimacy of its theatre origins. With a script stripped to the bare essentials, it has no other goal that to display the interaction between four characters over an 80-minute span, and in this it succeeds nicely. The only weakness, is Foster's performance, which rises too abruptly to hysteria and sometimes seems forced, and it's a bit hard to swallow that John C. Reilly's character is married to Foster's. Nonetheless, I found this a worthwhile black comedy and I laughed a lot.

This was really good and different, but it did have some very dark elements to the story simply because of the time in which it was set.<br /><br />Bernie Gunther has every right to be depressed.  He's just recently completed a military assignment in Russia where he has seen the wholesale murder of Jews by the German military.  In fact, he actually participated in some of those horrific acts of rounding the people up and then being present and participating in their deaths.  When he is able to get back to Berlin and start doing police detective work again he welcomes anything that will take his mind off what he witnessed in Belorussia and anything that might take his mind off committing suicide.  He spends his nights contemplating exactly how he could kill himself.  While investigating the apparent murder of a man who was eventually run over by a train Bernie is later able to rescue a woman being attacked in approximately the same location.  Maybe by working on the case, tweaking the noses of the officials in the police department, and enjoying the company of a beautiful woman Bernie will find a way to put off that suicide one more day.<br /><br />As you can tell from my synopsis of the plot, this is a very dark mystery.  It takes place during parts of 1941 and 1942 and travels from Berlin into newly occupied Prague which the Germans will no longer allow to be called Czechoslovakia.  In the wake of the German victories people are not even allowed to keep the names of their own towns or countries and citizens are rounded up wholesale for slaughter.  The Author's Notes at the end of the story let you know that many of the people you have been reading about were real people and the incidents of terrible inhumanity were actually true.  I have to tell you that it made for a rather depressing reading experience and yet the author created such complex characters and political situations that I really couldn't stop reading.  I understand that this is the eighth story in this series and I almost wish I had read at least the first one before I tackled this.  There are references to past people and events which, while I could understand they were from different novels, would have probably made a little more sense to me if I had been more familiar with the series.  I jumped into the series with book number eight and survived, you can too.  I must say though that the author had Bernie be such a flagrant anti-Nazi that it seemed to me that he went a little too far with that concept.  I understand that Bernie did not agree with the policies being put into existence, but would a citizen really have been allowed to speak so openly and plainly to Gestapo officers and to the SS Generals he met during his investigation?  The privations and hardships of Berliners were so vividly described that I couldn't help but feel sorry for them until I realized all the horrors their fellow countrymen were engaged in.  The atmosphere of this novel is exceptional.

1400032717|4.0|Christopher Boone, who identifies himself as a socially-hopeless fifteen year old mathematician, writes an account of a portion of his young life that occurred after finding the neighbor's black poodle skewered with a garden fork. On the encouragement of one of his teachers, Siobham (pronounced Shi--like in ship--Vaughn, stress on the second syllable, very close sound to chiffon), he decides to write a murder mystery novel about finding the killer of that dog, as if he were Sherlock Holmes, a favorite problem solver of his. As a consequence of his investigation, however, he uncovers confusing and disturbing information about his neighbors and his family.<br /><br />Don't look for chapter one in this book, as Christopher uses the first twenty-three prime numbers (2, 3, 5, 7, 11, ...) to order his chapters. Christopher does math problems in his head any time he needs internal peace from anxiety, fear, frustration, or claustrophobia. Based on some of the confusing and disturbing information he sleuthed, he and his pet rat, Toby, and using his dad's cash card, head from Swinden, his home town, to London, on a trip he had never made, on a train which he had never ridden, and through strange places he had never been.<br /><br />The reader encounters British figures of speech, like \\"losing one's rag\\" (losing one's temper), or \\"having a pig of a day,\\" or \\"take-away chips.\\" (These last two weren't explained, but the first means \\"having a very difficult day\\", and the second means \\"French fries\\"). Metaphors confused Christopher because he saw the world as a dichotomy of facts and lies. His brain thought in precise terms, like \\"I stepped back two meters\\" or he wore \\"brown shoes that have approximately 60 tiny circular holes in each of them.\\"<br /><br />Christopher knew many facts about diverse scientific and geographical issues and inserted them into his story, almost helter skelter, but they were always tied tangentially to the line of the story, or, at a minimum, to a word inthe previous sentence. That he called his story a mystery novel was surprising, since he thought novels \\"were lies about things that didn't happen\\" and he was investigating a real murder.<br /><br />This is a good read, a short read, a quick read. The adult reader will get a beautiful but painful sense of what it is like to be the parent of an autistic or Asperger's child. YA readers will get a lot of insight into how the minds of autistic or Asperger's kids work; it might help them be more understanding, more tolerant, and even more helpful to those kids.  Try renting the audio book from the library to get a better sense of the British context.
B006OGMEUI|5.0|It's important to note that Helvetios is a concept album. What this means is that the entirety of the album is to be taken together as a piece that attempts to paint a larger picture - with each song being an individual portion of that picture.<br /><br />For Helvetios, the album as a whole is a journey, the story of the Helvetii - ancestors of the Swiss from Ancient Roman times - told from their point of view as they fought against the might of the Roman legions, as led by Caesar and as later documented by Caesar in his \\"Commentaries on the Gallic War\\". (Fun fact: 'Eluveitie' is derived from an early piece of graffiti meaning 'Helvetian'). The prologue and epilogue speak of war, and individual songs are named after battle locations and take the listener along the Helvetians' journey as they fight for a place to live, settle, and ultimately come to terms with their demise.<br /><br />At its core, Helvetios is still very much Eluveitie.<br /><br />Without getting into specifics of individual songs, Helvetios exhibits something of an evolution. A lot of people will be wary when reading that Eluveitie may have changed their style with this latest album, and upon first listen, the album may seem to be a bit disjointed, with rougher transitions than previous albums, and less folk melodies. While this is all somewhat true, given a few more listens from beginning to finish, and the genius in the way they've laid out the last journey of an extinct culture begins to rise beyond a slight unfamiliarity with the style.<br /><br />It feels that Eluveitie is trying to tell a story first and foremost with this album, beyond even trying to make amazing music (which there is still quite a bit of). It's a story that has been forgotten by history, and the way that Eluveitie brings it back to life with Helvetios is something that should really be appreciated. The album as a whole may lack the singularly enthralling singles that previous albums were full of - although \\"A Rose for Epona\\"and \\"Uxellodunon\\" come close while being very fresh stylistically, and \\"Santonian Shores\\" brings classic Eluveitie - and some people may write this album off for it. But taken as a whole for the story that it attempts to tell, this is Elueveitie celebrating their folk origins through the hills and valleys of their culture and history and it's quite worthy, both for the story it paints and for the music it presents.

B004P8JPS6|4.0|In a lot of ways, The Affair plays out as an origin story for Jack Reacher, telling the story of Reacher's last major investigation as part of the United States Army: a series of dead women in a small Southern town with a very large Army base population. Like the best Child books, The Affair never neglects Reacher's brawling abilities (though his conflict with some local brothers feels like a bit of an afterthought, given how quickly it disappears from the story), but its primary focus is always on the central mystery and Reacher's keen, probing investigatory skills. The case here is relatively simple and (a little disappointingly) clean-cut; while there are some intriguing developments and surprises, ultimately this one unfolds more simply than many of the best Reacher books. What it has going for it, though, is the nice sense of seeing where Reacher's tics and habits come from. The Affair wouldn't be half as involving for a newcomer to the series, but as a long-time fan of the books, Child does a nice job balancing the seeds for Reacher's future life with the ongoing story, never letting the book turn into a thudding foreshadowing job (like, say, Lucas did in the prequels). The Affair isn't up there with the best Reacher books (sad, since it breaks the streak of 61 Hours and Worth Dying For), but it's still a solid, enjoyable read, and another great example of how really talented, clever, and smart Child is at creating fascinating characters and gripping situations.

0316017930|4.0|A Review of Outliers By Malcolm Gladwell<br /><br /> Pico della Mirandola wrote a discourse entitled Oration on the Dignity of Man; in the article he wrote about the innate ability that humans have when it comes to destiny. He believed that one of the most unique attributes that set humans apart from all other species was the ability to decide their own fate. Gladwell discusses this topic frequently in his book, Outliers: A Story of Success. Gladwell sets the story by giving the definition of an outlier; noun, (1) Something that is situated away from or classes differently from a main or related body. (2) A statistical observation that is markedly different in values from the other of the sample. Then he continues with a summary, \\"This book is about outliers, about men and women who do things that are out of the ordinary. Over the course of the chapters ahead, I'm going to introduce you to one kind of outlier after another: to geniuses, business tycoons, rock stars, and software programmers. We're going to uncover the secrets of a remarkable lawyer, look at what separates the very best pilots from pilots who have crashed planes, and try to figure out why Asians are so good at math. And in examining the lives of the remarkable among us-the skilled, the talented, and the driven- I will argue that there is something profoundly wrong with the way we make sense of success.\\" He then proceeds to tell you the many different stories of success and how there really is no such thing as a self-made man. This book held me captivated throughout the entirety of it's whole. It gives a unique perspective of the human condition and what is wrong with the majority of modern day society.  Gladwell believes that it is what you do with the opportunities that are presented to you that determine success and not just dumb luck.

As he digs around, he soon discovers the stories were true, with very dangerous consequences for our young hero.  The first 30 or so pages are needed to build up steam, once Riggs finds his voice, however, the story moves at a decent clip.  What reeled me in, however, was the brilliant way in which the photos were incorporated into the story line.  I'll make another pitch to my (now 11 year-old); its an intriguing read.

0307719219|5.0|\\"Why Nations Fail\\" by Daron Acemoglu and James Robinson is an outstanding book about why some nations succeed, and others fail.  Acemoglu and Robinson discuss two different types of economies -- extractive and inclusive -- and explains why the extractive economies eventually fail, while inclusive ones tend to do well for many years.<br /><br />Basically, it all comes down to one thing: if a nation's politics allow people and institutions to prosper, they are inclusive.  If a nation's politics try to keep the already-rich elites in power while extracting every last cent from everyone else, they are extractive.  A nation can go from one to the other, but usually what happens is that an extractive economy (think \\"China,\\" \\"Mexico,\\" or most of Latin America) will succeed for a time; then, because too many people are shut out from the process, _and_ because there is a lack of what's called \\"creative destruction\\" (meaning there's a lack of innovation, because innovative products create both winners and losers and extractive economies won't gamble on anything even if the upside far outweighs the downside), they eventually stall out or fail (as we now see in Russia).<br /><br />Acemoglu and Robinson use many examples drawn from recent and ancient history to explain why this simple definition works.  Because if the economy in question is extractive, it doesn't matter if you've raised all sorts of interesting monoliths like the Mayans; eventually, it will fall.  And if the economy is inclusive, it will likely become more inclusive -- though there have been some times in the past where countries have \\"gone in reverse\\" and have become extractive after being inclusive, which causes nothing but pain for all concerned (save the elites, who are making out like the 1890s Gilded Age robber barons).<br /><br />Political processes, you see, are far more important than they seem.  That's why a country that favors innovation, like the United States has traditionally done, is an inclusiveeconomy where more people are able to make a living and be able to get their needs met; it's also why an extractive economy, like the former Soviet Union, eventually stalls out and ends up having to re-make itself both politically and economically.<br /><br />This is a highly readable, intelligent book about things that matter: politics.  The economy.  Education (an extractive economy doesn't want most of its people educated, because they might become innovators and unleash \\"creative destruction\\"). And even philosophy.<br /><br />\\"Why Nations Fail,\\" in short, is an excellent book that is witty, understandable, and interesting about a subject that most people (if asked) would probably say bores them to tears.  It should be read by everyone who has an interest in politics, the world economy, history, or just about anything else.<br /><br />Five stars.  Highly recommended.<br /><br />Barb Caffrey

The geologists arrived, the Indian activists began to stir, and the government sent its people.  There were stirrings in the Sigma Forces and The Guild, opposing entities.  Young Navaho Indian , Kai, would do the bidding of WAHYA, \\"a militant Native American rights group.\\"  She would destroy the opening of the cavern and rebury their people.  The explosion rocked the entrance, but it wasn't of her making.  The geothermal springs moved the ground creating unfathomable spasms.<br /><br />In Washington, D.C. Painter Crowe, commander of Sigma Force's special service reviewed the reports coming in.  One sentence caught his attention and chilled him to the core:  \\"'The ground ... it looked like it was dissolving away.'\\"  At the Kamioko Observatory in Japan they began to observe unusual bursts of neutrinos around the world.  They pinpointed massive bursts that Dr. Riku Taneka though were geoneutrinos.  Something was terribly wrong.  The secrets of the fourteenth colony, The Devil Colony, were coming to the surface.  Six million people had died before Jefferson's time, but this time would be different.  No doubt, \\"a doomsday clock was ticking downward, one neutrino at a time.\\"  Would two opposing forces join together or would that clock keep on ticking?<br /><br />This is a an amazing Sigma Force novel that will keep the reader breathlessly on edge.  This novel was fascinating on many levels as it expertly hopped around the world from one operative group or small faction of innocent people to its evil ones.  The author seamlessly wove the narrative, one that traveled through centuries, funneling down to a doomsday scenario that was totally entrancing.  Everything from secret operatives, Native American lore, ancient secret societies, to the mythical Devil Colony added to the excitement of the tale.  For me, this was a pick up and put down novel.  Initially it was one that held some interest, but once the action started to wind down in the last hundred pages or so, I was hooked.  It didn't spin out of control, but rather spiraled and spun with excitement as all the elements of good, bad, indifferent, and evil roared to an exciting conclusion.  This is one sizzling novel that will spur everyone's imaginations ... can this stuff really happen? B006DQ1J0K|5.0|Leontyne Price and Carlo Bergonzi are well known in these roles, and both are magnificent here in 1961, before Price's vocal crisis (brought on by attempting Minnie in Puccini's \\"Fanciulla del West\\") robbed her of some of the sublime freedom she displays on this afternoon. Giorgio Tozzi is not quite in their league, but he is very fine here, caught in his full prime and in very good voice; later in his long career the tone would display more than a little rust. The really commanding performance here is Cornel MacNeil's as Charles V. In Act III he shakes the rafters with \\"O de verd'anni miei\\" and leads the great ensemble that follows with an \\"O sommo Carlo\\" worthy of Warren. Even if the others were not in their best voice (and they all all) this set would be worth having for MacNeil alone. The sound is very clear and full-bodied, although there is a little interference (alas, in Act III) but it's not enough to mar this splendid performance. As another reviewer has aptly pointed out, Corelli and del Monaco are the benchmark interpreters of the title role. Here Bergonzi, spurred on by MacNeil in an exceptional state of grace, challenges them. This is molton Verdi, pouring forth a flood of melodies, a truly profligate display of invention, sung by great voices who happen to be great singers as well, under a fiery young (gay) American conductor, Thomas Schippers. Verdi would be very pleased.
